Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with

Dissertation Task 2 2 2360921 
 
ABSTRACT 
 
Multimorbidity (MM), the presence of two or more chronic conditions, poses growing challenges for 
public health systems. While extensive research exists on single chronic diseases, limited work has 
examined the role of diet, particularly dietary inflammati on, in predicting multimorbidity. This study 
explores the impact of dietary inflammation, as measured by the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII), and 
its relationship with multimorbidity, alongside demographic factors such as BMI, age, gender, and 
ethnicity. Using data from 6,655 participants from the NHANES 2015-2018 dataset, the study applies a 
combination of exploratory analysis, logistic regression, and machine learning models to predict 
multimorbidity. The primary objective is to evaluate the potential of  DII and demographic variables in 
identifying individuals at risk for multimorbidity, a condition increasingly associated with poor health 
outcomes and higher healthcare burdens.  
 
Exploratory analysis revealed significant associations between DII quartiles and demographic variables, 
including gender, ethnicity, BMI, and chronic conditions. Statistical tests such as Chi -Square and 
ANOVA were used to identify these relationships, whil e Mann-Whitney U tests highlighted significant 
differences in BMI and DII between individuals with and without multimorbidity. The analysis also 
showed that certain nutrients, such as total fat, omega 3 , MUFA, PUFA,  saturated fatty acids , and 
vitamins (A, D, and B12), were not significantly different across multimorbidity groups, suggesting a 
lesser role in predicting multimorbidity association. Logistic regression models demonstrated that DII 
is a significant predictor of multimorbidity, but its predictive power is substantially enhanced when 
combined with demographic variables. Interaction terms showed that the effect of DII on 
multimorbidity risk is influenced by age and BMI, refining the predictive model further. To predict 
multimorbidity, four machine learning models —decision tree, random forest, neural networks, and 
XGBoost—were implemented.   XGBoost performed slightly better than the other models (75.96%), 
although Random Forest (0.749%) and Neural Networks (0.756%) also showed strong predictive power, 
with BMI emerging as the significant predictor across all models. Subgroup analysis indicated that BMI, 
age, DII, and ethnicity were critical factors in predicting multimorbidity, with the highest predictive 
accuracy observed in the non-Hispanic Black subgroup. 
 
The findings contribute to public health research by demonstrating that DII, particularly when 
combined with demographic factors, is a significant predictor of multimorbidity. The study also found 
that the DII has more predictive power compared to broader dietary measures, such as fruit and 
vegetable consumption in machine learning models. The application of machine learning techniques, 
especially XGBoost, Random Forest, and Neural Networks, highlights their potential for use in public 
health prediction models aimed at early identification of individuals at risk for m ultiple chronic 
conditions. Subgroup analysis emphasised the importance of demographic -specific interventions, as 
predictive accuracy was lower in older populations, highlighting the need for targeted prevention 
strategies. These findings could inform pers onalised nutrition programs and public health to reduce 
multimorbidity risk.

CHAPTER 1: INTRODUCTION 
The relationship between food and health is complex , having been the subject of extensive 
research over the years (Willett, 2013) . It is widely acknowledged that our dietary choices 
significantly influence our overall health and play a crucial role in preventing and treating 
various disorders  (Sandua, 2023). Chronic inflammation has been linked to numerous 
illnesses, including diabetes, heart disease, and several forms of cancer, making it a common 
factor in many health issues affecting our society. While inflammation is the body’s natural 
response to  injury or illness (Ricker & Haas, 2017), prolonged inflammation can severely 
damage bodily systems (Sears, 2015).  An anti -inflammatory diet, rich in whole foods like 
fruits, vegetables, whole grains, healthy fats, and lean proteins, has been proposed as a 
solution to reduce inflammation and lower the risk of chronic illnesses (Sandua, 2023; English 
et al., 2021). Characterised by antioxidants, phytochemicals, and omega-3 fatty acids, this diet 
can effectively combat systemic inflammation, making it a powerful tool in managing chronic 
diseases (Sandua, 2023; Chen et al., 2022). 
1.1 Multimorbidity and Its Growing Impact 
While there is extensive research on single chronic diseases and the role of diet in managing 
these conditions, there is limited research on understanding the relationship between diet and 
multimorbidity. The presence of two or more chronic medical conditions in one individual is 
known as multimorbidity (MM) or multiple long -term conditions (MLTC) (Johnston et al., 
2018; Ho et al., 2022). Healthcare systems worldwide often focus on single -disease 
comorbidities, rendering the management of multiple chronic c onditions ineffective. An 
estimated 51% of people over 60 have multiple long-term diseases, with a global incidence of 
MLTC estimated at 37.2% (Chowdhury et al., 2023). In the UK, the prevalence of two or more 
chronic health disorders among those over 65 is predicted to rise to 67.8% by 2035 (Kingston 
et al., 2018). These statistics depict the increasing strain multimorbidity places on public 
health systems and the need for effective management strategies. 
1.2 Challenges in Predicting and Managing Multimorbidity 
Predicting multimorbidity is challenging, especially as life expectancy rises and the population 
ages (Skou et al., 2022) . The condition’s complexity , where patients often have multiple 
interrelated chronic diseases, complicates accurate prediction (Delanerolle, 2021). Identifying 
modifiable risk factors is difficult due to the interactions between conditions and their varying 
effects across populations (Zhang et al., 2022). Recent advancements in machine learning 
(ML) offer promising solutions, enabling sophisticated tools to uncover patterns and improve 
prediction accuracy by accounting for factors like diet, age, gender, and ethnicity ( Polessa 

--- PAGE 7 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 7 2360921 
 
Paula et al., 2022). The  need for early detection and intervention is highlighted by  the 
detrimental effects of multimorbidity on health, including lower quality of life and increased 
healthcare expenses (Bricca et al., 2023). ML models must also be adaptable to diverse 
populations, especially in low- and middle-income countries, where health inequalities and 
limited resources present unique challenges (Mohan et al., 2022). Therefore, improving the 
accuracy and applicability of these models is crucial for effective, personalised care. 
1.3 Importance of Diet in Influencing Inflammatory Processes and Multimorbidity 
Diet plays a crucial role in the development and management of multimorbidity, with available 
studies demonstrating the impact of dietary patterns on the risk of multiple chronic 
conditions. Research has shown that higher adherence to healthy dietary patterns, such as the 
Alternate Healthy Eating Index-2010 (AHEI-2010) and the Mediterranean Diet, is associated 
with a lower incidence of chronic diseases and long-term multimorbidity development (Vega-
Cabello et al., 2024). Conversely, the Western dietary pattern has been linked to an increased 
risk of multimorbidity, while adherence to patterns rich in white meat and prudent food 
choices has shown inverse associations with multimorbidity risk ( Zhang et al., 2022). 
Additionally, specific food groups like fish, fruits, and cereals have been found to decrease the 
risk of multimorbidity, emphasising the importance of dietary interventions in preventing and 
managing multimorbidity (Zhang et al., 2022).  
1.4 Research Problem and Objectives 
This study aims to examine the impact of dietary components on inflammation and 
multimorbidity by calculating the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and considering 
demographic factors such as age, gender, BMI, and ethnicity. Additionally, it addresses the 
gap in the application of machine learning techniques to predict multimorbidity based on 
dietary and demographic data. The research questions are: 
• How does dietary inflammation, as measured by DII, impact multimorbidity in adults? 
• Can the risk of multimorbidity be reduced by anti-inflammatory diets? 
• Can machine learning models predict multimorbidity based on DII and other relevant 
factors using the NHANES dataset? 
1.5 Significance of the Study  
Given the rising prevalence of multimorbidity and its association with poor quality of life and 
increased healthcare burden, understanding the dietary factors that contribute to 
inflammation and chronic disease s is crucial. This study aims to contribute to this

understanding by using National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey (NHANES)  data 
to develop a reliable prediction model by implementing strong statistical and machine 
learning techniques to detect individuals at risk of developing multimorbidity . The research 
seeks to improve early intervention and targeted prevention strategies, providing deeper 
insights into the complex interactions between diet, demographics, and multimorbidity. The 
insights gained could guide dietary recommendations and public health strategies, ultimately 
reducing the burden of multiple long-term conditions (MLTC). 
1.6 Structure of the Dissertation 
The introduction chapter has laid the groundwork by outlining the research background, 
problem statement, objectives, and the significance of the study.  The next chapter r eviews 
existing literature on diet, inflammation, the Dietary Inflammatory Index, chronic conditions, 
multimorbidity, and advancements in machine learning, also identifying gaps in the literature. 
Methodology chapter describes the research design, data sources, and provides a 
comprehensive data cleaning structure, statistical analysis, clustering, and machine learning 
model development, including the subgroup group analysis for multimorbidity prediction. The 
results chapter presents the findings from the statistical analysis, clustering, and predictive 
modelling efforts. We further interpret these results, discussing their implications for both 
theory and practice in the discussion chapter. The conclusion chapter summarises the study's 
contributions, limitations and provides suggestions for future research.

CHAPTER 2: LITERATURE REVIEW 
2.1 Inflammation and Its Impact on Health  
The body uses inflammation as a defence mechanism against foreign substances, infections, 
damaged cells, and irritants (Sandua, 2023). This involves a microcirculation response where 
serum proteins and leukocytes move from the blood to affected tissues ( Sandua, 2023), 
resulting in symptoms like heat, redness, swelling, and pain (Serhan et al., 2010; Libby, 2008). 
Acute inflammation, marked by these symptoms, is the initial phase, facilitating the delivery 
of nutrients and immune cells to fight infections and aid tissu e healing. In contrast, chronic 
inflammation occurs  due to prolonged exposure to inflammatory triggers such as stress, 
pollution, chemicals, or an unhealthy diet (Ricker & Haas, 2017). Unlike acute inflammation, 
chronic inflammation progresses slowly , affecting multiple body systems, and is associated 
with high levels of pro -inflammatory markers like interleukin -6 (IL -6), C -reactive protein 
(CRP), and tumour necrosis factor -alpha (TNF-alpha) (Serhan et al., 2010 ). This persistent 
inflammation can contribute to the development of various chronic diseases, including 
diabetes, cardiovascular disease (CVD), arthritis, and some cancers (Sears, 2015; Libby, 2008; 
Sandua, 2023). Controlling chronic inflammation is crucial for preventing these diseases and 
maintaining homeostasis and overall health (Serhan et al., 2010; Sears, 2015). 
2.2 Dietary Factors Modulating Inflammation 
Extensive research highlights the significant role of diet in modulating the body's 
inflammatory response (Sears, 2015 ).  A diet high in whole, unprocessed foods that reduces 
inflammation can help prevent chronic inflammation and advance general health and wellness 
(Zwickey et al., 2019). However, the relationship between diet, inflammation, and disease is 
complex, influenced by other factors such as environmental triggers, lifestyle choices, and 
genetics (Ricker & Haas, 2017; Sandua, 2023). Nevertheless, new treatment strategies 
targeting inflammatory pathways have become possible because of our growing understanding 
of the role inflammation plays in the pathophysiology of illness. 
2.2.1 Anti-Inflammatory Diets and Chronic Disease Prevention 
An anti-inflammatory diet is one such strategy that has attracted a lot of interest lately due to 
its possible health advantages. It has been established that specific nutrients within these diets 
minimise the likelihood of acquiring certain chronic diseas es and lessen chronic 
inflammation. These diets emphasise foods rich in anti -inflammatory nutrients like 
carotenoids, flavonoids, and omega-3 fatty acids (Zwickey et al., 2019; Sandua, 2023). Studies 

--- PAGE 10 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 10 2360921 
 
on Mediterranean and Okinawan diets, which are high in these anti -inflammatory nutrients, 
demonstrate significant reductions in inflammatory markers and a corresponding decrease in 
chronic disease incidence (Ricker & Haas, 2017; Giugliano et al., 2006; Zwi ckey et al., 2019). 
Conversely, diets high in trans fats, processed carbohydrates, and sugars increase pro -
inflammatory cytokines, contributing to chronic inflammation and related diseases (Giugliano 
et al., 2006). 
In a thorough analysis of the literature, Casas and Estruch (2016) investigated the connection 
between certain foods, nutrients, and dietary patterns with chronic inflammatory illnesses. 
They found that unhealthy diets high in red and processed meats, sweets, refined grains, and 
fats are correlated with elevated plasma inflammatory biomarkers, which in turn raise the risk 
of chronic diseases such as  cardiovascular disease (CVD),  cancer, and type 2 diabetes . 
Conversely, healthier dietary patterns, like the Mediterranean diet, have been connected  to 
decreased inflammation and a lower chance  of developing these illnesses. Additionally, 
excessive consumption of s aturated and trans fats and omega -6 fatty acids was found to 
increase inflammation, while nutrients like omega -3, polyunsaturated fatty acids ( PUFA), 
vitamin E, and polyphenols have anti -inflammatory effects. The review focusses on the  
protective benefits of the Mediterranean diet and the detrimental effects of Western dietary 
habits, explaining the urgent need for public health policies that promote healthier eating 
habits for the prevention of chronic diseases (Casas & Estruch, 2016). Further studies reinforce 
the benefits of anti -inflammatory diets in lowering the risk of certain cancers (Tabung et al., 
2017) and preventing conditions such as obesity (Sears, 2015), asthma (Alwarith et al., 2020), 
and COPD (Scoditti et al., 2019; Chen et al., 2022). Moreover, diets high in omega-3 fatty acids 
may provide protective benefits against neurodegenerative diseases like Alzheimer's (Sandua, 
2023).  
 
These studies point out the importance of adopting dietary patterns that reduce inflammation, 
offering a comprehensive approach to controlling the progression of chronic diseases, 
strengthening the immune system, and enhancing longevity (Sandua, 2023). 
2.2.2 Development of Dietary Inflammatory Indices  
The Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) was developed through a thorough analysis of 1 ,943 
publications by Shivappa et al. (2013) to evaluate a diet's likelihood of causing inflammation. 
By assigning a score to dietary components based on how they affect inflammatory markers 
including TNF -alpha, IL -6, and CRP, the DII enables standard comparisons between 
populations. This instrument essential for evaluating how nutri tion affects chronic 
inflammation and associated health consequence s has been validated for use in 

--- PAGE 11 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 11 2360921 
 
epidemiological studies to predict the risk of multimorbidity (Shivappa et al., 2013).  To 
increase the performance of the DII, the Energy-Adjusted Dietary Inflammatory Index (E-DII) 
was introduced, which determines scores based on each 1,000 kilocalories consumed utilised 
with the global database's energy -standardized version.  This adjustment enables a more 
accurate assessment of dietary inflammation across diverse populations, accounting for 
changes in total caloric intake (DiNatale et al., 2023). 
Several studies have evaluated the performance of both the DII and E-DII in predicting 
chronic low -grade inflammation, as indicated by increased plasma concentrations of 
inflammatory markers. For instance, a study conducted in Scotland on the elderly revealed a 
positive correlation between higher E-DII scores and circulating levels of IL -6 and CRP  
(Hariharan et al., 2021) . Similar associations have been observed in other populations, such 
as Portuguese adolescents, where higher DII scores were linked to inc reased IL -6 and 
complement component C4 levels (Hariharan et al., 2021).  
2.2.3 Relationship between Chronic Disease and Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) 
Hariharan et al. (2021) reviewed the relationship between the DII  and health outcomes, 
focussing on type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM), obesity, and cardiovascular diseases (CVD). The 
review indicated a strong correlation between high DII scores (pro -inflammatory diets) and 
increased risks of obesity, T2DM, and CVD, while also discussing a link between anti -
inflammatory DII scores and a reduced 20 -year risk of developing T2DM . The review 
concluded by calling for more interventional studies to determine if reducing diet -induced 
inflammation can effectively lower chronic disease risk (Hariharan et al., 2021). 
Extended research by Puddu et al. (2020) provided extensive evidence linking  dietary 
inflammation to mortality  and cardiovascular disease (CVD) . Using cohort data from seven 
countries, they examined the relationships between E -DII scores and death rates using 
multiple linear regression models and Pearson correlation coefficients, controlling for 
common risk factors such as age, blood pressure, cholesterol levels, smoking habits, and social 
class. Higher E -DII scores were shown to be negatively connected with age at death and 
positively correlated with increased risks of death from CVD and all causes. These findings 
indicate that higher levels of dietary inflammation are linked to earlier mort ality, calling 
attention to the role of anti -inflammatory diets in reducing the risk of chronic illnesses and 
extending life expectancy (Puddu et al., 2020). 
In a related domain, Mignogna et al. (2022) conducted a cross-sectional study using data from 
Southern Italy to examine the relationship between food processing, dietary inflammation, 
and low-grade inflammation. They measured dietary inflammation using the E -DII for four 

--- PAGE 12 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 12 2360921 
 
food groups (processed culinary ingredients, processed foods, ultra -processed foods (UPF), 
and unprocessed/minimally processed foods). The relationships between food processing 
levels, E -DII scores, and a composite low -grade inflammation score (INFLA -score) were 
assessed using multivariable -adjusted linear regression models. The results of the study 
showed that minimally processed foods were connected to lower E -DII scores and decreased 
inflammation, whereas higher intake of processed and UPF was linked to increased low-grade 
inflammation. This suggests that UPF may have pro -inflammatory properties, emphasising 
the need to limit their consumption to reduce the risk of chronic diseases (Mignogna et al., 
2022). 
These studies demonstrate how the DII and E-DII effectively capture the relationship between 
diet and the development of chronic conditions. They suggest that adopting an anti -
inflammatory diet can significantly reduce the risk of chronic diseases, improve  mortality 
rates, and promote overall health.  
2.3 Multimorbidity: Current Trends and Challenges   
King et al. (2018) conducted a cross-sectional analysis to examine variations in multimorbidity 
among US adults using NHANES data (1988-2014). It was found that the proportion of 
individuals with multiple illnesses rose from 45.7% to 59.6 % between 1988 and 1994.  The 
highest prevalence was observed among adults aged 65 and older (91.8%), with women and 
non-Hispanic Black and White populations particularly affected. Obesity was identified as a 
major factor contributing to the increasing trend in multimorbidity (King et al., 2018).  
 
Further exploring the role of ethnicity and BMI, Botoseneanu et al. (2022) employed a 
longitudinal cohort study using data from the U.S. Health and Retirement Study (1998–2016), 
focussing on individuals who were between 51 and 55 years old at the start.  The study 
examined the accumulation of seven chronic illnesses across different BMI categories, with a 
focus on variations by race/ethnicity. According to the study, non -Hispanic White people 
(70.9%) were less likely to be overweight or obese than non -Hispanic Black participants 
(82.3%) and Hispanic participants (78.9%). Moreover, Black individuals accumulated chronic 
illnesses more rapidly and reached the multimorbidity threshold 5 –6 years earlier than their 
White counterparts (Botoseneanu et al., 2022).  
 
Liang et al. (2023) also utilized NHANES data (1999–2018) to examine lifestyle trends among 
US adults with CVD, particularly focussing on obesity, physical inactivity, and nutrition. They 
observed a decline in poor nutrition from 47.5% to 37.5% but a significant increase in obesity 
rates from 32.0% to 47.9%. Trends in physical inactivity and smoking remained relatively

Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 13 2360921 
 
unchanged. Notably, non -Hispanic Black individuals and those with lower socioeconomic 
status exhibited higher rates of obesity and physical inactivity (Liang et al., 2023). 
From a global perspective, Skou et al. (2022) provided a comprehensive analysis of the 
underlying mechanisms and management challenges  connected with multimorbidity.  The 
study emphasized that multimorbidity is more prevalent in socioeconomically poor groups 
and affects around one -third of the global population. The three main drivers of 
multimorbidity are ageing, lifestyle -related risk factors, and socioeconomic conditions. The 
research describes the inadequacies of current healthcare systems, which often focus on single 
diseases, leading to fragmented care (Skou et al., 2022). 
Overall, these studies reveal the growing challenge of multimorbidity, with age,  body mass 
index (BMI), ethnicity, and socioeconomic status all playing critical roles in its prevalence and 
progression. While King et al. (2018), Botoseneanu et al. (2022), and Liang et al. (2023) 
provide insights into demographic variations in the United States, Skou et al. (2022) offers a 
broader global perspective on the socioeconomic drivers of multimorbidity. These s tudies 
highlight the critical need for targeted interventions, integrated, patient-centred care models, 
and systemic healthcare reforms in order to successfully lower the socio -economic cost of 
multimorbidity, especially for vulnerable populations (Skou et al., 2022). 
2.3.1 Relation of Diet in Managing Multimorbidity 
While previous studies in this research have demonstrated the effectiveness of anti -
inflammatory diets in the prevention and management of individual chronic conditions, their 
potential to address the intricate interactions between multiple chronic conditi ons requires 
deeper understanding. 
Ruel et al. (2014) conducted a longitudinal study involving Chinese individuals to investigate 
the relationship between diet and multimorbidity.  The status of eleven chronic illnesses was 
evaluated in the trial at baseline and five years later using three-day weighted dietary records. 
Analyses using ANOVA, Pearson correlation, and paired t -tests revealed that healthier stages 
of multimorbidity wer e linked to increased intakes of fruits, vegetables, and whole grains. 
Specifically, the consumption of grains o ther than rice and wheat, which are rich in dietary 
fibre and essential micronutrients, was linked to a lower incidence of multimorbidity. This 
study demonstrates the importance of a balanced diet rich in anti -inflammatory foods for 
managing and potentially preventing the progression of multimorbidity (Ruel et al., 2014). 
Zhang et al. (2022) expanded on this by investigating the relationship between food habits and 
multimorbidity using a prospective cohort study involving adults from the UK Biobank. Using 

--- PAGE 14 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 14 2360921 
 
cox regression models to evaluate hazard ratios and confidence intervals, they identified three 
eating patterns: western, white meat, and prudent. The western pattern was linked to a higher 
risk of multimorbidity. Conversely, a lower risk was associated with the prudent pattern, which 
is high in fruits, vegetables, and grains. Fish and poultry that were somewhat adhered to the 
white meat pattern also showed preventive properties  against multimorbidity. Additionally, 
individuals who developed multimorbidity tended to  be male, older, less affluent, less 
educated, smokers, non-drinkers and reported lower physical activity levels and higher BMI. 
These findings provide robust evidence that dietary patterns play a significant role in the 
development and management of multimorbidity (Zhang et al., 2022). 
Further evidence from Spain, involving older adults (65 years and above) , reinforces the link 
between diet quality and multimorbidity. The study  found that adherence to the 
Mediterranean Diet Adherence Screener (MEDAS) and Alternate Healthy Eating Index -2010 
(AHEI-2010) was linked to fewer chronic illnesses and cardiometabolic diseases.  Higher 
compliance with the AHEI-2010 was linked to a lower baseline number of cardiometabolic 
diseases and overall chronic illnesses, as well as a slower pace of chronic disease accumulation 
over time. Similarly, stronger compliance with MEDAS was associated with fewer overall 
chronic diseases and neurodegenerative disorders. Key factors contributing to these outcomes 
included moderate alcohol intake, low trans -fat consumption, reduced intake of sugar -
sweetened drinks, and high fruit consumption, emphasising the importance of hig h-quality 
diets in addressing multimorbidity in senior populations (Vega-Cabello et al., 2024). 
 
Additionally, in a study from southern India, Mahajan et al. (2024) explored the relationship 
between dietary inflammation and multimorbidit y. Using the DII based on 27 food 
parameters, they found a significant positive association between higher DII scores and the 
prevalence of multimorbidity. The use of Poisson regression revealed a dose -response 
relationship, indicating that dietary improvements could significantly reduce multimorbidity. 
Notably, higher DII scores were primarily due to low consumption of anti -inflammatory 
nutrients rather than high intake of inflammatory ones, underscoring the need for nutrient -
rich diets. Interestingly, there was no significant interaction between DII and sex in relation 
to multimorbidity, indicating that the effect of dietary inflammation on multimorbidity 
appears to be similar across both genders. This study aligned with research from high-income 
countries, explaining the global importance of dietary inflammation and its management 
across different socioeconomic contexts (Mahajan et al., 2024). 
 
Collectively, these studies signify the critical role of dietary patterns /nutrients and 
inflammation in the management of multimorbidity. Moreover, the global relevance of these 

--- PAGE 15 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 15 2360921 
 
findings is evident, with research conducted in both high -income (UK, Spain) and lower -
middle-income (southern India, China) settings, highlighting the widespread impact of diet 
quality and inflammation on the development and advancement of multiple long -term 
conditions (MLTC). 
2.3.2 Advances in Machine Learning for Multimorbidity Prediction 
 
Advancements in machine learning (ML) techniques have shown significant promise in the 
prediction and management of multimorbidity, offering new insights and tools for healthcare 
professionals. Delanerolle et al. (2021) discussed how  ML can enhance risk assessment and 
disease classification , particularly in  automating the identification of multimorbidity . The 
study discussed that ML tools can serve as diagnostic, prognostic, and treatment aids, if 
challenges related to feasibility, validity, fidelity, data security, and interpretability are 
adequately addressed. Delanerolle  et al. (2021)  emphasised the advantages of using 
algorithms trained on large, representative datasets to achieve high -accuracy predictions, 
which are less prone to cognitive biases and human errors . The study also stressed on the 
importance of real -time clinical application, model interpretability, ethical  considerations, 
data quality, and empirical validation (Delanerolle et al., 2021). 
 
Additionally, Polessa Paula et al. (2022) assessed the performance of various ML  algorithms 
in predicting multimorbidity using data from the ELSA -Brasil study, which included 15,105 
participants and 31 predictor variables including alcohol, coffee, fruits , and vegetable 
consumption. Using random forest  (RF) and support vector machines (SVM) as base 
classifiers, the study tested seven multilabel classifiers, with the classifier chain with random 
forest (RF -CC) emerging as the best -performing model , demonstrating that RF -based 
methods outperform SVM -based methods in classifying multiple diseases . The top 15 most 
significant predictors included BMI, systolic and diastolic blood pressure, age, and sex, while 
fruit consumption was the least important feature.  This study proposes that broad dietary 
measures like fruit and vegetable consumption may not adequately capture the complexity of 
diet-related risks for multimorbidity (Polessa Paula et al., 2022). 
 
In another study, Uddin et al. (2022) utilized network analytics and ML to predict the 
probability of multimorbidity and the co -occurrence of major chronic diseases, based on 
Australian healthcare administrative data . They analysed data from 123,983 patients, 
including 1.93 million events with 25 comorbidities. The study employed five  ML models 
(logistic regression, k-Nearest neighbours, naive Bayes, random forest, and extreme gradient 
boosting), incorporating patient and network features. The findings indicate that the accuracy 
range for models that solely use patient features is 62.19% –75.39%, whereas the accuracy 

--- PAGE 16 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 16 2360921 
 
range for models that solely use network features is 62.11% –70.88%. Both are lower than 
models combining patient and network features (62.81% - 95.05%), with extreme gradient 
boosting achieving the highest accuracy throughout. This study demonstrated the potential of 
combining patient and network characteristics to enhance multimorbidity prediction (Uddin 
et al., 2022).  
 
These studies highlight the capability of machine learning to revolutionize the prediction and 
treatment of multimorbidity . By utilising advanced algorithms and large datasets, these 
approaches improve risk assessment accuracy, uncover complex disease patterns, and enable 
personalized healthcare. Nonetheless, issues such as data quality, model transparency, and 
ethical concerns need to be resolved to facilitate the responsible use of these technologies in 
clinical settings. 
2.4 Summary and Recurring Limitation 
The literature emphasises the crucial impact of diet on the onset of chronic conditions and 
multimorbidity. An anti-inflammatory diet has proven effective in preventing and managing 
these conditions. However, the rising incidence of multimorbidity poses major challenges to 
healthcare systems across the globe. As populations age and lifestyle -related risk factors  
increase, governing the complex interplay of these conditions becomes essential.  In addition 
to increasing healthcare utilisation and expenses, multimorbidity also results in a higher rate 
of hospital admissions and a lower standard of living. E thnic minorities face an even greater 
burden, underlining the need for comprehensive and integrated healthcare strategies, 
including weight management, lifestyle modifications, and targeted public health 
interventions. Improvements in ML techniques have demonstrated potential for forecasting  
health outcomes, such as  multimorbidity. These advanced algorithms can examine  large 
datasets to identify hidden patterns and relationships that conventional statistical methods 
might miss. However, while there is a growing body of literature exploring the application of 
ML in healthcare, there remains a notable gap in research specifically focused on utilising 
dietary factors, specifically the Dietary Inflammatory Index , to predict multimorbidity.  The 
DII serves as a valuable tool for assessing the inflammatory potential of an individual's diet, 
which has been linked to various chro nic diseases. Despite its relevance, few studies have 
combined DII calculations with demographic factors such as gender, age, BMI , and ethnicity 
in the context of multimorbidity prediction.  
 
This gap highlights a substantial opportunity for future investigation. The study aims to 
address this by using ML to predict multimorbidity based on DII as a more precise measure of 
dietary inflammation. This approach offers a deeper evaluation of how diet, combined with

Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 17 2360921 
 
demographic factors, contributes to the risk of multimorbidity, thereby enhancing our 
understanding of these influences.  This study may help develop focused interventions and 
enhance the health for a diverse population.

CHAPTER 3: METHODOLOGY  
3.1 Research Design and Ethics 
This cross-sectional study conducted a secondary data analysis. The study adhered to ethical 
principles, as confirmed by the BREO application, which determined that the project does not 
require a full ethical review. The confirmation letter is included in the appendix section.   
3.2 Data Collection 
The data for this study was sourced from the National Health and Nutrition Examination 
Survey (NHANES), carried out by the National Center for Health Statistics (Jin et al., 2022).  
Every two years, NHANES gathers health-related data from a multistage, stratified probability 
sample of the noninstitutionalized U.S. population .  Survey participants are selected using a 
stratified probability design, ensuring a representative sample (Liu et al., 2024). Every 
NHANES dataset has complete descriptions of sampling and design that are publicly  
accessible (O'Dwyer et al., 2023). The NHANES study methods were approved by the Centers 
for Disease Control and Prevention Institutional Review Board, and all participants completed 
written informed consent forms (King et al., 2018).  To boost sample sizes, this study merged 
NHANES 2015-2016 and NHANES 2017-2018 data after some primary cleaning. To create the 
study population, demographic, questionnaire, examination, and dietary information from 
these two NHANES cohorts were taken out and merged.  Two 24-hour dietary recall interviews 
were used in NHANES to a ssess dietary consumption data. Estimates of calorie, nutritional, 
and other food component intakes were made from the type s and quantities of foods and 
beverages ingested in the 24 hours preceding the interviews (Jiang et al., 2022). All the open 
data was obtained from the official NHANES website (Nhanes - National Health and Nutrition 
Examination Survey homepage, 2024). 
3.3 Assessment of dietary inflammatory index (DII) 
While the Energy-Adjusted Dietary Inflammatory Index (E -DII) offers a refined approach by 
accounting for variations in caloric intake, this study opted to use the original Dietary 
Inflammatory Index (DII) due to data availability constraints. The E-DII requires access to an 
energy-adjusted global database, which is not publicly available. Given these limitations, the 
DII was selected as a viable and equally robust alternative for assessing dietary inflammation. 
The DII has been extensively validated and is widely used in epidemiological studies to predict 
the risk of chronic diseases, making it a suitable choice for this research  (Hariharan et al., 
2021). Dietary parameters were scored based on their impact on inflammatory biomarkers

(zero for no impact, negative for anti-inflammatory, and positive for pro-inflammatory effects) 
(Ruiz-Canela et al., 2016). Of the 45 potential food parameters used to calculate the DII, 28—
including total energy, total fat, carbohydrates, fatty acids  (saturated, monounsaturated  
(MUFA), and polyunsaturated (PUFA)), protein, alcohol, thiamin, vitamins (A, B2, B6, B12, 
C, D, and E), niacin, fibre, cholesterol, iron, magnesium, zinc, selenium, folic acid, beta -
carotene, caffeine, omega-3 and omega-6 fatty acids —were included for the analysis as these 
were the only available nutrients  in NHANES. Although fewer than 30 nutrients were used, 
the DII scores are still considered valid (Liu et al., 2021; DiNatale et al., 2023).   The method 
developed by Shivappa et al. (2014) was applied in the following way to calculate the DII: 
1. The nutrient intake for each 24-hour recall were first averaged.

3.4 Data Preparation 
Data from the NHANES website was loaded into the R environment, where essential libraries 
and packages were used for data modification, visualization, and analysis. This process 
involved cleaning and merging data to create a comprehensive dataset for predictive 
modelling. 
3.4.1 Chronic disease assessment  
Data on a range of chronic illnesses was gathered through self-reported means by NHANES.  
Participants were asked if a doctor or other health professional had ever diagnosed them with 
conditions such as diabetes, hypertension, stroke, etc (Jiang et al., 2022). In this study, eleven 
chronic illnesses were considered: diabetes, asthma, arthritis, COPD, cancer, stroke, 
hypertension, coronary heart disease (CHD), liver disease, failing kidneys, and obesity. These 
conditions were binary coded as absent ('0') or present ('1'). Obesity was defined as a body 
mass index (BMI) of 30 kg/m² or higher , and hypertension was based on a  diastolic blood 
pressure of 80 mmHg or more and  systolic blood pressure equal to or exceeding 130 mmHg 
(Bryan et al., 2021). 


--- PAGE 22 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 22 2360921 
 
3.4.2 Multimorbidity Classification 
For the machine learning classification task, individuals in the NHANES dataset were 
categorised into two groups . Positive class if individuals have two or more diseases 
(multimorbidity) and negative class for fewer than two diseases (zero or one). 
3.4.3 Covariates  
The study considers demographic factors such as age, ethnicity, BMI, and gender that may 
influence the course of multimorbidity, as identified in prior literature (Botoseneanu et al., 
2022; King et al., 2018; Liang et al., 2023). Additionally, age of onset for a chronic condition 
was also included for analysis that were available in NHANES dataset.  
3.5 Data Cleaning 
The data cleaning process for the NHANES dataset was methodically carried out in three key 
stages to ensure accuracy for analysis. Initially, the downloaded .xpt NHANES data files were 
converted to .csv files for easier manipulation in R. Relevant variables from each dataset were 
selected using the “SEQN” variable as the key and merged using an inner join . The entire 
process is illustrated in the flowchart included in each section, detailing every step-in detail. 
3.5.1 Specific Cleaning Before DII Calculation 
 
 
 
Before calculating the DII, the dataset underwent rigorous checks for implausible values and 
significant outliers in nutrient intake data for both day 1 and day 2 using boxplots. Outliers 
were removed from beta-carotene, vitamin A (RAE), with a tighter cut-off (4000 kcal for men 
and 3500 kcal for women) considered for energy  intake based on research by Willett (2013). 


--- PAGE 23 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 23 2360921 
 
This ensured that the nutrients intake data remained within a typical range, minimising the 
effect of extreme values . This stage also included multicollinearity checks, flagging variables 
with correlations above 0.90 for potential model stability issues. The Spearman test identified 
three pairs of variables ( MUFA and total fat (0.961), total saturated fatty acids and total fat 
(0.903), and omega-6 fatty acids and total fat (0.99)) exceeding this threshold. Since 
correlations close to 0.99 indicate near-perfect collinearity, omega-6 fatty acids were excluded 
from the DII calculation, reducing the number of nutrients used to generate DII to 27. 
Additionally, adjustments were made to the caffeine intake unit to make it consistent with the 
global database . The DII calculations were performed as described in section 3.3 after 
confirming that there were no missing values in the nutrients data.  
3.5.2 Cleaning Before Combining NHANES 2015-2016 & NHANES 2017-2018 
 
 
 
The NHANES 2015 -2016 and 2017 -2018 datasets were cleaned separately to account for 
differences in available variables. Invalid values were replaced with not available (NA), and 
chronic diseases, gender, and ethnicity were re -coded. Age of onset variables with more than 
50% missing data were excluded before merging the dataset s, resulting in the removal of all 
age of onset variables, which limits the analysis of disease onset patterns. The final combined 
dataset NHANES 2015-2018 included 7,297 observations and 51 variables.

3.5.3 Cleaning After Combining NHANES 2015-2016 & NHANES 2017-2018 
 
Post-combination, BMI was calculated, and checks confirmed that no respondents were under 
18 years of age, so no age values needed adjustment. Hypertension was identified averaging 
three blood pressure readings, while the four th reading was excluded due to more than 75% 
missing values. The age variable was converted from numerical to categorical. This conversion 
was necessary because NHANES encoded individuals aged 80 and above as "80," while others 
were recorded with their exact age. To maintain consistency, age intervals were defined as 
follows: Young Adult (18 -34 years), Middle Aged (35 -54 years), Senior (55 -74 years), and 
Elderly (75+ years).  Categorical and binary data were converted to factors.  A total of 6 42 
observations with missing data for chronic conditions and demographic variables were 
excluded. Chronic condition count variable was created to categorise individuals based on the

number of chronic conditions. This thorough process ensured that the final dataset was well -
prepared for analysis, with 6655 observations and 47 variables.  
3.6 Statistical Analysis   
This section outlines the various statistical methods employed in the study, including 
numerical and categorical exploratory data analysis, DII exploration, multimorbidity 
exploration, DII quartile and multimorbidity analysis, preliminary modelling, clustering, and 
machine learning classification prediction modelling. A p-value of less than 0.05 was used to 
determine statistical significance for all analyses. 
3.6.1 Numerical Analysis 
We conducted a detailed numerical analysis, starting with univariate measures such as mean, 
variance, and standard deviation. This was followed by visual exploration using histograms 
and Q -Q plots to assess distribution, central tendency, and normality. To validate these 
observations, the Kolmogorov -Smirnov test was performed, comparing each variable's 
distribution to a normal curve and calculating p-values for significance. For bivariate analysis, 
we used Spearman correlation coefficients for its ability to detect monotonic relationships  to 
examine relationships between variables, visualising the results with a heatmap . Pairs with 
high correlations (coefficients > 0. 80) were identified alongside their p -values for further 
review. Scatter plots were employed to explore potential linear or non-linear associations and 
outliers, enhancing our understanding of variable interactions. 
3.6.2 Categorical Analysis 
We explored categorical variables through visualisation and summary techniques. Initially, 
univariate analysis was performed, generating bar plots to display the distribution of 
categories for each variable. Summary statistics were conducted to understand the frequency 
and proportion of each category. Bivariate analyses were explored using mosaic plots, helping 
to identify potential patterns between variables. 
3.6.3 Boxplot Analysis of Numerical and Categorical Relationships 
The relationship between numerical and categorical variables was visualised using boxplots. 
This approach provided a clear understanding of how variables varied in terms of spread, and 
possible outliers. 
 
 

--- PAGE 26 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 26 2360921 
 
3.6.4 DII Exploration Analysis 
We categorized the DII into quartiles and generated summary statistics and box plots to assess 
DII distribution within the quartiles. Based on Wang et al. (2024) method we examined the 
relationships between DII quartiles and other variables in our dataset (Wang et al., 2024). 
This involved calculating proportions, performing Chi-Square tests for categorical variables , 
and conducting ANOVA for continuous variables like BMI  across the quartiles. This step-by-
step exploration enabled us to understand the potential impact of DII on multimorbidity and 
related factors within our dataset. 
3.6.5 Multimorbidity Exploration Analysis 
To compare the distributions of numerical variables (DII, BMI, and nutritional intake 
measures) between individuals with and without multimorbidity, we used Mann -Whitney U 
tests, which adjust for non-normality in the data. The resulting p-values provided insights into 
which variables significantly differed between the multimorbidity groups. Chi-Square tests 
and p -values helped to investigate the association between categorical variables and 
multimorbidity status.  
3.6.6 DII Quartiles and Multimorbidity Analysis  
We separately explored the relationship between DII quartiles and multimorbidity, using a 
Chi-Square test to assess whether there was a statistically significant association between 
these variables. Additionally, a boxplot of DII quartiles by multimorbidity status was created 
for visualizing the distribution and comparison.  
3.7 K- Means Cluster Analysis 
 
 


--- PAGE 27 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 27 2360921 
 
K-Means clustering was implemented using the Orange 3.37.0 software. The data was pre-
processed by normalizing the columns to ensure comparability across features. The K -
Means++ algorithm was used for initialization, with the model set to re-run 10 times to ensure 
stability and consistency in the results. The silhouette score, used to compare an object's 
alignment with its assigned cluster versus other clusters, helps determine the ideal number of 
clusters by evaluating a range between two and seven  clusters. Further analysis using 
silhouette plots and box plots was performed to analyse the clustering results. 
3.8 Preliminary Model Fitting  
Before proceeding to the classification modelling, we conducted a simple logistic regression  
preliminary model to gain insights into the predictive power of various variables for the 
outcome of multimorbidity. This step helped identify the most influential variables and 
interactions, guiding the subsequent, more complex modelling efforts. 
 
Three models were performed as shown in the flow chart . For each model , metrics such as 
McFadden's R -squared and Akaike Information Criterion (AIC) were calculated to assess 
model fit.

3.9 Classification Modelling 
 
 
The primary aim of this study was to provide a reliable model to predict multimorbidity using 
the DII and relevant covariates through machine learning techniques. The dataset was split, 
with 70% designated for training and 30% for testing . We implemented four widely used 
machine learning models, experimenting with different combinations of features, including 
DII (as both a continuous variable and in quartiles), age category, BMI (both as a continuous 
variable and categorized), gender, and e thnicity. The models were selected based on their 
proven performance in the literature for predicting complex conditions like multimorbidity : 
Decision Tree for its interpretability, Neural Networks (NN) for their effectiveness in handling 
complex data as reviewed by Delanerolle et al. (2021), Random Forest (RF) for its robustness 
and accuracy in multimorbidity prediction as shown by Polessa Paula et al. (2022), and 
Extreme Gradient Boosting (XGBoost) for its high predictive accuracy, as evidenced by Uddin 
et al. (2022). The models were trained on the training data, cross -validated, and then tested 
using the test data . This approach allowed us to identify the most effective feature sets for 
prediction. The best-performing model, determined by evaluation metrics, was subsequently 
applied for subgroup analysis, providing insights into whether the accuracy of multimorbidity 
prediction varied across different demographic subgroups. A brief explanation of the model's 
functionality is provided below. 


--- PAGE 29 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 29 2360921 
 
3.9.1 Decision Tree 
Algorithms like Decision Trees construct a tree -like model by repeatedly splitting data into 
subsets based on input features. They offer intuitive visual representation, ease of 
interpretation, and flexibility without requiring assumptions about data distribution. They are 
also well -suited for real -time predictions since they are typically easier to train than more 
intricate algorithms (CS5706, Lecture 7). 
3.9.2 Random Forest 
Random Forest (RF) operates as an ensemble method by generating multiple decision trees 
from randomly selected subsets of data and features, then combining their outcomes to 
enhance prediction accuracy . It improves accuracy and robustness by reducing overfitting, 
handles both classification and regression tasks, and provides insights into feature 
importance, which can be valuable for feature selection and understanding the model's 
behaviour (CS5706, Lecture 7). 
3.9.3 Neural Networks 
Neural Networks (NN) consist of interconnected layers that process data through weighted 
combinations and non-linear activation functions, effectively capturing complex patterns. NN 
models excel at capturing non -linear relationships in data, which  can be challenging for 
traditional models  but require substantial data and computational resources. While less 
interpretable, they are highly flexible and perform well across various tasks (CS5706, Lecture 
6).    
3.9.4 Extreme Gradient Boosting 
By combining predictions from several weak models, XGBoost is an optimised gradient 
boosting algorithm that generates a strong predictive model. It is well -known for its 
exceptional performance on structured data, outperforming other classification algorithms.  It 
is scalable, efficient, and can handle big datasets with ease.  
3.9.5 Cross validation and tuning the Models 
Each model was fine -tuned by adjusting hyperparameters, such as the number of trees and 
folds, using cross-validation to achieve optimal performance. 
3.9.6 Model Evaluation and Selection 
Model evaluation and selection are crucial steps to ensure that the chosen model performs well 
on the data and generalises effectively to new, unseen data. In this section, we will describe

of their performance (CS5706, Lecture 9).  
• Confusion matrix provides a clear visuali sation of the actual vs. predicted

and where it is making mistakes. 
 
• A model's accuracy is measured by the percentage of correct predictions it makes,

• A receiver operating characteristic (ROC) curve graphically illustrates the relationship 
between the true positive rate (sensitivity) and the false positive rate (1 - specificity) 
across different threshold levels. The model's performance across all thresholds is 
summarised by a single scalar value called Area Under the Curve (AUC). With values 
ranging from 0.5 (random performance) to 1 (perfect performance), AUC offers a 
thorough assessment of model performance.

CHAPTER 4: RESULTS  
In this chapter, we present the results from the exploratory data analysis, statistical tests, 
cluster analysis , and machine learning modelling conducted on the NHANES 2015 -2018 
dataset. This chapter begins by presenting the descriptive statistics for the data, which helps 
to understand the overall structure and distributions of the data. Following this, we have 
divided the data into numerical and categorical analysis, followed  by the DII and 
multimorbidity analysis  and the clustering results . Finally, the chapter concludes with the 
preliminary modelling, machine learning classification, and subgroup analysis evaluation. 
4.1 Exploratory Data Analysis 
After performing the extensive cleaning on the data set as explained in the methodology, the 
final dataset, “NHANES_final_completed,” consists of 6655 unique observations without any 
missing values.  
4.1.1 Analysis of Numerical Variables 
• Univariate Analysis: 
 
The distribution of DII in the dataset shows that values range from -5.58 to 4.97, with a mean 
of 1.14, indicating a skew towards positive DII values. This suggests that most of the population 
has a pro -inflammatory diet. The variance of 3.18 indicates some variability in dietary 
inflammation levels, which could be crucial in studying its association with chronic diseases.

28.94. The variance of 45.51 reflects a moderate spread in BMI values, highlighting variability

Following the univariate analysis, we plotted Q-Q plots and conducted a Kolmogorov-Smirnov

nutrient-intake measures were not normally distributed (p < 0.05). This finding is particularly 
relevant as it influenced the choice of statistical tests applied in further analysis. However, 
since the primary focus of the predictive model is on DII and BMI , both of which are almost 
normally distributed, these additional nutrient variables were explored for insights but were 
ultimately excluded from the predictive model, as DII already integrates these nutrients. This 
exclusion was done to maintain focus on the primary research question and to ensure the 
model's robustness by relying on variables with clearer, more interpretable relationships.

4.1.4 Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) Analysis 
In this section, the analysis of the Dietary Inflammatory Index was conducted by dividing the 
DII values into four quartiles based on the 25th, 50th, and 75th percentiles. This enabled us to 
understand how varying levels of dietary inflammation relate to demographics, and chronic 
conditions. The dataset revealed that 4,830 participants had a pro -inflammatory diet (DII > 
0), while 1,829 participants had an anti -inflammatory diet (DII < 0) , with no participants 
having a neutral DII (DII = 0). The DII quartiles were categorized as follows: Q1 (DII ≤ -0.073) 
representing primarily anti-inflammatory diets; Q2 ( -0.073 < DII ≤ 1.378) covering slightly 
negative to slightly positive values; Q3 (1.378 < DII ≤ 2.534) indicating moderately pro -
inflammatory diets; and Q4 (DII > 2.534) representing the most pro-inflammatory diets. Each 
quartile contained approximately 1,664 participants, ensuring a balanced distribution.

obesity.

4.1.5 Multimorbidity Analysis

Due to the non-normality of many variables (especially nutrients), the Mann-Whitney U test 
was conducted to assess whether there is a statistically significant difference in the distribution 
of numerical  variables between participants with and without multimorbidity. Significant 
differences were found in BMI and DII (p < 0.05). Additionally, 19 out of the 27 nutrients used 
to calculate DII showed highly significant p -values, indicating strong differences between 
multimorbidity groups. However, nutrients like total fat, MUFA, PUFA, saturated fatty acids,

•  Chi-Square Test  
 
 
To evaluate the association between multimorbidity status and categorical variables, a Chi -
Square test was applied. The relationship between multimorbidity, chronic illnesses, and 
demographic characteristics is displayed in the bar plot. Arthritis shows the highest 
association, followed closely by hypertension and obesity. Age is also a significant factor . 
Diabetes, cancer, and asthma also show significant associations, indicating that these 
conditions contribute substantially to the multimorbidity burden.  
 
These results underline key conditions and demographic factors that are significantly 
associated with multimorbidity, informing potential areas of intervention and further 
research.

4.2 K-means Cluster Analysis 
The silhouette score peaked at 0.168, indicating that the most logical grouping of the data was 
into two clusters. The quality of the clustering was not enhanced by adding more clusters and 
doing so added needless complexity. Consequently, two clusters that offered the most 
insightful data segmentation were chosen for the final analysis. It is crucial to remember that 
a silhouette score of 0.168 is low, indicating that clustering may not be the best strategy for 
this dataset as the data may not be well separable. 
   
• Multimorbidity status across clusters 
 
 
 
We plotted multimorbidity status across the identified clusters and observed that Cluster (C1) 
exhibits a higher prevalence of multimorbidity, while Cluster (C2) has a lower prevalence. C2 
exhibits a more distinct separation and a predominant 'no' multimorbidity category, indicating 
that this group may be made up of people with better general health or fewer health risks. In 
comparison, C1 exhibits a mix, but a higher percentage of ‘yes’ multimorbidity category, 
suggesting a higher risk of illness within this cluster. The statistical significance of these 
differences was confirmed by the Chi -Square test, which also brought attention to the clear 
differences in multimorbidity between the two clusters. 
 
•  Ethnicity, gender, and age across clusters

4.3 Preliminary Model Fitting: logistic regression 
 
In Model 1, t he positive coefficient for DII (0.09235) indicates that as DII increases, the log -
odds of having multimorbidity also increases. With a p -value of 1.09e -12, this association is 
statistically significant . However, DII alone was a weak predictor with a McFadden’s R² of 
0.006 and an AIC of 9177.5. In Model 2, even after adjusting for covariates such as age, BMI, 
gender, and ethnicity, the DII remains a statistically significant predictor of multimorbidity, 
with a coefficient of 0.0567. The analysis shows that age categories ( middle aged, senior), 
higher BMI, and being non-Hispanic Black or White  are significant predictors of increased 
multimorbidity risk. Males also show a slightly higher risk compared to females. Including 
covariates significantly improved model performance, as indicated by a McFadden’s R² of 
0.294 and an AIC of 6537.8. In Model 3, a significant positive interaction between DII and 
middle age, along with a significant negative interaction between DII and BMI, indicates that 
the impact of DII on multimorbidity is stronger in middle -aged individuals and weaker in 
those with higher BMI. The inclusion of these interaction terms led to marginal improvements 
in model performance, as evidenced by a McFadden’s R² of 0.297 and an AIC of 6528.9. 
The logistic regression modelling reveals that while DII predicts multimorbidity, its predictive 
power is weak when used alone. Including demographic variables improves the model, 
highlighting the complex relationship between diet, demographics, and multimorbidity.

4.4 Machine Learning Evaluation for Multimorbidity Classification 
This section discusses the results from the machine learning models (Decision Tree, Random 
Forest (RF), Neural Network (NN), and XGBoost) applied to predict multimorbidity, using the 
evaluation metrics outlined in the methodology. Four compositions of features were tested to 
determine the optimal features for accurately classifying individuals based on their likelihood 
of having multimorbidity. The test dataset contained 1997 observations and the train dataset 
contained 46 58 observations. The optimal feature  models were then discussed briefly.  To 
ensure robust performance and lower the risk of overfitting, cross -validation was 
implemented on all models.  
4.4.1 Decision Tree for Predicting Multimorbidity Based on Only DII 
 
 
 
A simple decision tree was initially constructed using only DII to predict multimorbidity. The 
tree split the data at a DII value of 1.9, distinguishing between individuals likely to have 
multimorbidity (DII ≥ 1.9) and those who are not (DII < 1.9). At 53.68% accuracy, the model 
showed limited predictive power . Sensitivity (41.92%) was lower than specificity (64.97%), 
indicating the model's reduced effectiveness in correctly identifying individuals with 
multimorbidity. 
4.4.2 Model Evaluation with Different Feature Composition 
Four different feature sets  were used to train and test the models, age category, gender, 
ethnicity, BMI (as a continuous variable and categorical),  and DII (as a continuous variable 
and quartiles). The performance metrics for each feature set are shown below in Table 4.5. 
 


--- PAGE 48 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 48 2360921 
 
Feature Set 1:  Prediction using BMI as a categorical variable ( underweight BMI < 18.5 , 
normal weight 18.5 ≤ BMI < 25, and overweight BMI ≥ 25), age, gender, DII (continuous), and 
ethnicity. 
Feature Set 2: Prediction using BMI as a continuous variable , age, gender, DII quartiles 
(categorical), and ethnicity. 
Feature Set 3:  Prediction using BMI as a category, age, gender, DII quartiles (categorical), 
and ethnicity. 
Feature Set 4:  Prediction using BMI as a continuous variable, age, gender, DII (continuous), 
and ethnicity. 
 
 
 
When comparing the different feature compositions, it is evident that feature set 2, using BMI 
as a continuous variable along with DII quartiles, consistently performs best across all metrics 
for all the 4 models, indicating this as the best feature composition. Using BMI as a continuous 
variable seems to capture more predictive information regarding multimorbidity,  improving 
model performance, though categorical BMI still yields good results with slightly lower AUCs. 
Incorporating BMI as a categorical variable alongside DII quartiles results in performance 
similar to  feature set 1 (where BMI is categorical), with decent but not optimal predictive 
ability. The final feature set with both BMI and DII as continuous predictors shows the best 
performance across neural network and random forest models however the XGBoost model

between dietary and demographic factors. Among ethnic groups, non-Hispanic Asian, non-

still some impact.

4.4.4 Subgroup Analysis 
For the  subgroup analysis, we selected the Random Forest model due to its robustness , 
particularly when using DII as a continuous variable. This feature set achieved an AUC of 0.82 
for the RF model ( Table 4.5), making it a strong candidate for further exploration across 
different subgroups. This analysis helped us identify whether the prediction of multimorbidity 
was more prominent in certain demographic subcategories, such as age (young adult, middle 
aged, senior, elderly) , gender, and ethnicity  (Mexican American, non -Hispanic White, and 
Black).

roles age plays in predicting multimorbidity. Although DII is generally less influential than

Table 4.6 and Figure 4.17 presents the evaluation metrics for the Random Forest model 
across various subgroups. For age, the model performed well for young adults (AUC 0.82) but 
had low sensitivity (0.458),  indicating difficulty in detecting multimorbidity,  while middle-
aged individuals showed better balance (AUC 0.80, sensitivity 0.718). Performance declined 
for seniors (AUC 0.75), and for the elderly, the model achieved perfect sensitivity (1.00) but 
no specificity, predicting all as multimorbid (AUC 0.59).  For ethnicity, the model performed 
best for non-Hispanic Blacks (AUC 0.86, sensitivity 0.899, specificity 0.647), followed by 
Mexican Americans (AUC 0.85) and non-Hispanic Whites (AUC 0.80). For gender, males had 
an AUC of 0.80 with higher sensitivity (0.804) than specificity (0.680), while females 
performed better overall (AUC 0.84) with balanced sensitivity (0.747) and specificity (0.759). 
The F1 scores show a good balance between precision and recall for most subgroups, but the 
model underperforms for the elderly and young adult subgroups. 
Overall, the analysis reveals that the model performs well in some subgroups, such as Non -
Hispanic Blacks and females, but struggles with others, particularly the elderly , highlighting 
the need for tailored predictive approaches to improve multimorbidity detection across these 
demographic groups. 
4.5 Summary  
In summary, the results from this analysis focusses on the relationships between dietary 
patterns, demographic factors, and multimorbidity risk. Exploratory analysis revealed 
significant associations between DII quartiles and demographic variables, like gender, 
ethnicity, BMI, and certain chronic conditions. The analysis also showed that BMI, DII, age, 
and some conditions were significantly different across multimorbidity groups , while certain 
nutrients, such as total fat, omega 3, MUFA, PUFA, saturated fatty acids, and vitamins A, D, 
and B12, did not, suggesting a lesser role in multimorbidity association.  The cluster analysis 
further differentiated the population into high -risk and low -risk groups, signifying the 
importance of BMI, DII, and demographic factors in shaping health outcomes.  The 
preliminary logistic regression models demonstrated that while DII alone is a statistically 
significant predictor of multimorbidity, its predictive power is substantially enhanced when 
combined with other demographic variables. The interaction terms showed how the impact of 
DII on multimorbidity varies with age and BMI, further refining the predictive model. The ML 
provided RF, NN, and XGBoost as good performing models, with XGBoost performing slightly 
better for predicting multimorbidity . The subgroup analysis using R F revealed that certain 
demographic factors, specifically BMI, age, DII, and ethnicity, play critical roles in predicting 
multimorbidity within specific subpopulations , achieving  the best performance for non-
Hispanic Black subgroup.

Overall, the results reinforce the significance of personalised healthcare strategies, including 
diet, demographic characteristics, and the potential of machine learning models for improved 
multimorbidity prediction accuracy.

CHAPTER 5: DISCUSSION  
5.1 Key Statistical Findings 
The results highlight the significance of dietary inflammation and the covariates (BMI, gender, 
ethnicity, age)  in the development of multimorbidity. The significant association between 
positive DII scores and multimorbidity  in this dataset is consistent with  existing literature 
linking pro-inflammatory diets to an increased likelihood of chronic disease risk (Mahajan et 
al., 2024). Although this association was statistically significant, the effect size remained 
moderate. This aligns with the understanding that while dietary factors play a crucial role in 
multimorbidity, it is not the sole determinant. Other factors, such as demographics, genetics, 
environmental exposures, and lifestyle choices, also significantly contribut e to the risk of 
developing multiple chronic conditions (Ricker & Haas, 2017; Sandua, 2023). This study 
contributes to this body of knowledge by demonstrating that, even after adjusting for age, BMI, 
gender, and ethnicity, DII remains a statistically significant predictor of multimorbidity. These 
results suggest that dietary interventions targeting inflammation could be effective in 
governing and reducing multimorbidity, highlighting the importance of integrating such 
strategies within broader health -promoting initiatives, as described by Skou et al. (2022).  
Although the clustering analysis revealed distinct groups, the low silhouette score suggests 
that the clusters were not well -separated, indicating that multimorbidity may involve a more 
complex interplay of factors than initially considered. Introducing ad ditional variables, such 
as behavioural or genetic factors, may enhance clustering and provide a clearer differentiation 
between high- and low-risk groups. 
5.2 Machine Learning  
The application of machine learning models in this study demonstrated their potential for 
accurately predicting multimorbidity. Random forest (0.749%), neural network (0.756%), and 
XGBoost (0.7596%) demonstrated strong performance using data such as BMI, DII, gender, 
ethnicity, and age. Our XGBoost model’s accuracy (75.96%) aligns with Uddin et al. (2022), 
where models using only patient features achieved 62.19% –75.39% accuracy. While the 
specific features differ with our study, the comparable accuracy levels reinforce the reliability 
of patient -level data in multimorbidity prediction. This consistency validates our findings 
while suggesting that, as Uddin et al.  (2022) demonstrated, integrating network -based 
features like degree centrality and PageRank scores offer insights into a patient's interactions 
with healthcare systems and social networks, potentially improving prediction accuracy in 
multimorbidity models. 
 

--- PAGE 57 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 57 2360921 
 
The random forest feature importance plots in this study revealed that BMI and age were the 
dominant predictors of multimorbidity . This is consistent with Polessa  Paula et al. (2022), 
where BMI and age were also among the top predictors of multimorbidity in their machine 
learning models. Both studies signify the critical role of BMI and age in predicting 
multimorbidity. Moreover, our analysis also revealed that treating BMI as a continuous 
variable captured more detailed predictive information regarding multimorbidity, leading to 
better model performance compared to treating BMI categorically. This suggests that 
preserving the granularity of measures like BMI is crucial in predictive modelling. 
 
The variable importance plots further  indicate that the DII also contributes to the role in 
predicting multimorbidity compared to broader dietary measures like fruit and vegetable 
consumption, which were included in the Polessa Paula et al. (2022) study. While they found 
that fruit consumption was among the least important predictors, our findings indicate that 
the DII, which captures the inflammatory potential of the entire diet, offers a more nuanced 
and impactful assessment of diet-related risks for multimorbidity. Among ethnic groups, non-
Hispanic Asian, White , and Black categories show higher importance. These findings echo 
previous studies that have shown racial and ethnic disparities in the prevalence and 
progression of chronic conditions across non-Hispanic White and Black categories 
(Botoseneanu et al., 2022; Liang et al., 2023). Male gender has a l esser but still some impact 
(Zhang et al., 2022). 
5.3 Subgroup Analysis  
The subgroup analysis highlights the varying predictive power of the Random Forest model 
across different demographic groups. BMI again emerged as a key predictor  across all the 
subgroups, especially for seniors, males, females, and non-Hispanic Black individuals, 
reinforcing the strong link with prior studies that have identified obesity as a key driver of 
multimorbidity (King et al., 2018; Botoseneanu et al., 2022) . The varying importance of age, 
across gender and non-Hispanic White and Black groups, reflects the complex ways that age-
related risk factors interact with other variables. While DII played a smaller role, its increased 
influence in subgroups like seniors , young adult s and non-Hispanic Blacks suggests that 
dietary inflammation still contributes to multimorbidity risk, especially in these populations. 
The limited predictive power of ethnicity and gender across subgroups suggests that while 
these factors do influence multimorbidity, their impact may be secondary to factors like BMI, 
age, and diet.  
The model performed best for non-Hispanic Black s ubgroup (AUC 0.86), suggesting that 
factors like BMI, age, and DII are more strongly correlated with multimorbidity in this group. 

--- PAGE 58 ---
Investigating the Impact of Dietary Inflammation on Multimorbidity Using the Dietary Inflammatory Index (DII) and Predicting Multimorbidity with 
Machine Learning: NHANES 2015-2018 
  
Dissertation Task 2 58 2360921 
 
However, the model struggled with specificity, particularly in older adults, where a high 
baseline prevalence of chronic conditions made it harder to distinguish between 
multimorbidity and non -multimorbidity. This suggests that multimorbidity risk factors are 
not uniform across populations, indicating a need for more refined predictors or additional 
features to improve performance in this group. Overall, our analysis stresses the importance 
of tailoring interventions to specific demographic groups and suggests future research should 
focus on validating these patterns in broader populations and on enhancing the model's ability 
to balance specificity and sensitivity for each subgroup. 
5.4 Summary  
Overall, this research talks about the critical role of dietary inflammation, as measured by DII, 
alongside the key demographic factors in predicting multimorbidity. While the DII is a 
significant predictor, BMI emerged as the most dominant factor across all models and 
subgroups, highlighting the connection between obesity and chronic diseases. The study aligns 
with existing literature on the importance of diet and demographic variables, although the 
effect size was moderate, indicating that multimorbidity is influenced by a combination of 
factors beyond diet alone. Machine learning models, particularly XGBoost, demonstrated 
strong predictive power, though incorporating network -based features, as seen in related 
studies, could further enhance accuracy. The subgroup analysis revealed variabil ity in model 
performance across demographic groups , emphasi sing the need for tailored public health 
interventions. Overall, this chapter highlights the strengths and limitations of the current ML 
models and suggests directions for future research to improve multimorbidity prediction using 
ML techniques.
